### 🤖 Assistant

Found the mechanism — and it's in the frenemy driver (`src/frenemy/driver.ts`, layer0), not the dashboard. Here's how it picks recipients (lines 300–363):

1. `busRoster({room})` → **every session in the room** that isn't tagged frenemy and isn't `exited` is treated as a "primary."
2. For **each** such session, it reads *that session's own* `hook_timeline`, diffs the files *that session* touched, critiques, and `busSend`s the finding with `to: primary.session_id`.

So a challenge is addressed to the session whose hook activity it reviewed. The "wrong session" comes from two real weaknesses:

- **The role-marker gap (known, flagged on #281/#282):** the launched frenemy doesn't get `PANOPTICON_FRENEMY_ROLE` set, so its *own* session has `role=null` and isn't `FRENEMY_FROM` (`"frenemy"`) — it's not filtered out, so the frenemy can review its own activity and challenge itself.
- **"Primary" = any non-frenemy session in the room** — including read-only ones like the `/panopticon review pr 282` session (that's exactly what got challenge id 7). It reviews their activity and addresses findings to them even though they aren't the agent doing the questionable edits. There's no notion of "who actually authored this change" vs "who's just present in the repo."

That's why your delivered challenge landed on the wrong agent: it went to whichever roster session's timeline the frenemy happened to review, not the intended editor.

**This is the other session's code** — the frenemy driver is being actively worked on right now (PR #282, and another session is in `src/service/direct.ts` as we speak). So I'd **coordinate the fix there**, not patch it from this Mission Control branch (I'd collide). The fix is on their side: (a) actually set/propagate the frenemy role marker so its own session is excluded, and (b) tighten "primary" selection (e.g., only sessions that actually authored edits in the window, or an explicit target).

Want me to (a) write this up as a handoff note for the frenemy owner (like the group-chat spec earlier), (b) dig into the *specific* message you triggered to pin which rule misfired, or (c) leave it — it's their layer?

### 🧑 User

well lets discuss for a minute. what do we think is the most correct solution? I don't think we should always assume that the session that made the code change is the only one who should fix it

### 🤖 Assistant

Good thing to slow down on — the targeting bug is really a symptom of a wrong mental model. Let me lay out how I'd think about it.

## The reframe
The current model is **"a finding belongs to its author, deliver it to that session."** Your instinct is right that that's wrong: a frenemy finding is about a **piece of code / a decision**, not about a person. Tying it to the author breaks in exactly the ways we saw — the author may be idle/exited (sits undelivered), attribution is unreliable (it hit a read-only review session), and in a shared workspace whoever's *active* is often the right one to act, not whoever typed it.

## What I think is most correct: scope the finding to the code, deliver point-of-use
Treat a finding as **attached to a code region**, and surface it to **whoever next touches that region** — regardless of who wrote it:

1. **Scope, not recipient.** The frenemy already cites locations (`src/frenemy/driver.ts:178-182`). So tag the bus message with `subject = "path:src/frenemy/driver.ts"` (the plan already has a generic `subject` field for exactly this), `to_session = NULL`.
2. **Point-of-use delivery.** When *any* live agent is about to `Edit`/`Write` that path (`PreToolUse`), the drain surfaces the matching open findings for that path — reusing panopticon's existing PreToolUse file-context injection seam. So the right agent gets it at the right moment, author or not.
3. **Room-visible always.** It still shows in the Mission Control feed immediately (and could become a PR comment) — so a human or any agent can see/act even if nobody edits the file again.
4. **Consume-once per (finding, session)** — using the per-recipient `agent_message_deliveries` table that just landed. An agent isn't re-nagged about the same finding, but a *different* agent touching that file still gets it. (This is exactly the group-chat fix's shape, applied to findings.)

That directly encodes "the author isn't necessarily the fixer": the finding rides with the code and lands on whoever works on it next.

## The nuances worth deciding
- **Author-as-context vs author-as-owner.** The author often has the most context, so it's reasonable to *also* send a directed nudge to the author *if still active* — but never *depend* on it. Optional, not load-bearing.
- **Not all findings are file-scoped.** "You deleted the flaky test to make CI pass" is a *decision*, not a region. Those don't have a clean path → fall back to **broadcast to the room** (every active agent drains once) or address the **currently-active** session. So really two lanes: `subject=path:*` (point-of-use) and `subject=null` decisions (broadcast).
- **Noise.** Point-of-use is self-throttling — a finding only surfaces when someone actually touches that code — which is much quieter than blasting every finding to every agent. That's a strong argument for scope-based over pure broadcast.
- **Staleness.** A finding about a region should expire/resolve when the region changes again (the diff that triggered it is gone) — otherwise a fixed issue keeps surfacing. Needs a "resolved when the path changes / on a newer finding" rule.

## My one-line recommendation
**Findings are scoped to code and delivered point-of-use to whoever next edits that path (consume-once per session), broadcast to the room for visibility, with a decision/broadcast fallback for non-file findings — author targeting becomes an optional nudge, never the dependency.**

This is the frenemy/bus owner's layer (they're mid-flight on `driver.ts`/`direct.ts` right now), so it's a design to hand them, not something I'd patch from the Mission Control branch. Want me to write this up as a concrete spec (schema use of `subject`, the PreToolUse path-match drain provider, the resolve-on-change rule) for them — the way I did the group-chat fan-out spec?

### 🧑 User

should we just inject into the hook that there are unread messages and then any session is free to read the messages and decide if they should fix them or not? maybe the agent that does fix it should mark the issue as resolved?

### 🤖 Assistant

Yes — I think that's *more* correct than forced point-of-use injection, and it's the cleaner model. You've basically described turning the bus into a **shared issue queue** for the room: notify → pull → decide → resolve. Two things it gets right that push-delivery doesn't: it respects **agent agency** (the agent reads and judges relevance instead of being force-fed), and it adds a **lifecycle** (open → resolved), which is exactly the shape of "the PR is effectively reviewed by the end."

A few refinements so it actually works:

**1. The nudge needs to be compact, not bare.** "You have unread messages" gets ignored. Inject a small *open-issues list* — `#7 src/frenemy/driver.ts:178 — settle timer unref'd` × N — so the agent can triage without reading. Full detail via `bus_read`. Keep it **idempotent**, not consume-once: it reflects the current open set every hook, so it persists until resolved (throttle re-injection to only when the open set *changes* for that session, via the existing dedup-per-session util, so it's not spammed every turn).

**2. Resolution = an append-only event + projection.** A `kind:"resolve"` message referencing the finding id (mirrors the planned claim/release shape). "Open findings" = challenges minus resolves, computed as a projection. Add a `bus_resolve(id, note)` tool. Then **any** session — or a human via Mission Control — can resolve. That fully decouples author from fixer, which was your whole point.

**3. The risk to design against: diffusion of responsibility.** "Anyone is free to fix" can mean *no one* does (everyone assumes someone else will). Cheap mitigations: an optional soft claim ("I'm taking #7"), or just lean on the human/active agent for the demo. Worth a conscious choice, not a silent gap.

**4. The killer addition — close the loop on resolve.** When an issue is marked resolved, the **frenemy re-reviews that region** and either confirms (stays resolved) or **reopens** it. That keeps "resolved" honest (an agent can't just claim it fixed) and makes the adversarial relationship actually adversarial through to the end.

**5. Point-of-use becomes an enhancement, not the baseline.** Baseline is the room inbox nudge. *On top*, if you're about to edit a file that has open findings, float those to the top of the nudge. Best of both.

Net model: **the frenemy files findings as open issues in the room; every session gets a compact "N open" nudge; any agent reads, decides, fixes, and marks resolved; the frenemy re-verifies on resolve.** Author targeting disappears entirely.

Ownership split: the drain-nudge + `bus_resolve` + the open-issues projection + re-verify loop are the **frenemy/bus layer** (their code, mid-flight) — I'd spec that for them. The **open→resolved burndown / issues panel is Mission Control** (mine) — and it's a great demo visual (watch issues open and burn down live).

Want me to (a) write the spec for the frenemy/bus side, (b) build the issues/burndown panel on the dashboard, or both?

### 🧑 User

I don't think we should make the messages too structured or mutable. the chat needs to be append only

### 🤖 Assistant

Agreed — keep the bus a plain **append-only chat**. Don't encode an issue tracker on top of it (mutable `resolved` flags, a `resolve` kind, open/closed projections). That's over-structuring, and it fights the append-only nature that makes the log durable and sync-friendly. Let me re-cut the model under that constraint:

**The bus is just a conversation. Messages are immutable. Triage and resolution are *emergent from reading the thread*, not stored state.**

- **Findings are chat messages.** The frenemy posts a finding. That's it — no subject-scoping required, no special lifecycle.
- **"Resolved" is just more chat.** An agent that fixes something posts "fixed the unref'd timer in driver.ts." The frenemy, reading the thread, replies "confirmed" or "still broken." Whether something is resolved is *understood by reading the conversation* — exactly like a human reading a channel — not a flag anyone mutates. LLM agents are good at this; it doesn't need to be machine-structured.
- **The only bookkeeping is append-only read-receipts.** To know what's *unread*, reuse the per-recipient `agent_message_deliveries` table that just landed — but reframe it as "I've seen this," recorded by **appending** a row. Messages themselves stay untouched.

**The one mechanic change to the drain:** instead of "inject challenge content and consume-once," the hook injects a lightweight pointer — *"N unread messages in your room — `bus_read` to see them"* — and **does not** mark them seen. The nudge persists until the agent actually reads. `bus_read` is what appends the read-receipts. So: notify (cheap, persistent) → agent pulls on its own judgment → reading marks seen → agent decides whether to act and, if it does, says so in chat.

That fully drops author-targeting (anyone reads, anyone fixes, anyone replies), keeps the chat append-only and unstructured, and the frenemy is just another participant that follows up.

The honest tradeoff: there's no cheap structured "3 open / 5 resolved" count — status lives in the prose. That's fine for agents, and for Mission Control it means we render the **chat thread** (which we do), not a burndown. I'd drop the burndown idea — it implied the structure you're rejecting.

This is still the frenemy/bus layer's code (the drain nudge + `bus_read` read-receipt semantics + the frenemy talking instead of directed-sending). Mission Control already just shows the append-only feed, so on my side nothing structural is needed.

Want me to write this up as the handoff spec for the frenemy/bus owner — "append-only chat + unread-pointer drain + read-receipts, no issue-tracker structure"?

The spec in one breath: **keep the bus a plain append-only chat; findings are room-broadcast messages (not addressed to an author); the hook injects a non-consuming "N unread → `bus_read`" pointer instead of force-feeding content; `bus_read` appends read-receipts (the existing `agent_message_deliveries`); resolution is just more chat that agents/the frenemy reason over — no `resolved` flag, no `resolve` kind, no projection, no mutation.**
